class="firstHeading" lang="en">Alternative medicine</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">Quackery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">Traditional medicine</a></div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:100%;"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Alternative medicine</a></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: lightgrey; font-family:monospace,monospace; font-size:0.9em;">\nAM, complementary and alternative medicine (CAM), complementary medicine, heterodox medicine, integrative medicine (IM), complementary and integrative medicine (CIM), new-age medicine, unconventional medicine, unorthodox medicine</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Altmed_function_2.svg" class="image"><img alt="Altmed function 2.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Altmed_function_2.svg/300px-Altmed_function_2.svg.png" width="300" height="553" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Altmed_function_2.svg/450px-Altmed_function_2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Altmed_function_2.svg/600px-Altmed_function_2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="346" data-file-height="638" /></a><div>How alternative treatments "work":<br />a) Misinterpreted <a href="/wiki/Regression_to_the_mean" class="mw-redirect" title="Regression to the mean">natural course</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the individual gets better without treatment.<br />b) <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">Placebo effect</a> or false treatment effect \xe2\x80\x93 an individual receives "alternative therapy" and is convinced it will help. The conviction makes them more likely to get better.<br />c) <a href="/wiki/Nocebo" title="Nocebo">Nocebo</a> effect \xe2\x80\x93 an individual is convinced that standard treatment will not work, and that alternative treatment will work. This decreases the likelihood standard treatment will work, while the placebo effect of the "alternative" remains. <br />d) No adverse effects \xe2\x80\x94 Standard treatment is replaced with "alternative" treatment, getting rid of adverse effects, but also of improvement.<br /> e) Interference \xe2\x80\x94 Standard treatment is "complemented" with something that interferes with its effect. This can both cause worse effect, but also decreased (or even increased) side effects, which may be interpreted as "helping".\n<br />Researchers such as <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology" title="Epidemiology">epidemiologists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clinical_statistician" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical statistician">clinical statisticians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacologists</a> use <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trials</a> to tease out such effects, allowing doctors to offer only that which has been shown to work. "Alternative treatments" often refuse to use trials or make it deliberately hard to do so.</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks vcard hlist" style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Fringe_medicine_sidebar" title="Template:Fringe medicine sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Fringe_medicine_sidebar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:Fringe medicine sidebar (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Fringe_medicine_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Alternative medicine</b>, <b>fringe medicine</b>, <b>pseudomedicine</b> or simply <b>questionable medicine</b> is the use and promotion of practices which are unproven, disproven, impossible to prove, or excessively harmful in relation to their effect \xe2\x80\x94 in the attempt to achieve the <a href="/wiki/Healing" title="Healing">healing</a> effects of <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>. They differ from <a href="/wiki/Experimental_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Experimental medicine">experimental medicine</a> in that the latter employs <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">responsible investigation</a>, and accepts results that show it to be ineffective. The <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a> is that alternative therapies either do not, or cannot, work. In some cases <a href="/wiki/Scientific_law" title="Scientific law">laws of nature</a> are violated by their basic claims; in some the treatment is so much worse that its use is <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">unethical</a>. Alternative practices, products, and therapies range from only ineffective to having known harmful and <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxic</a> effects.  \n</p><p>Alternative therapies may be <a href="/wiki/Regression_fallacy" title="Regression fallacy">credited</a> for perceived improvement through <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo effects</a>, decreased use or effect of medical treatment (and therefore either decreased <a href="/wiki/Side_effects" class="mw-redirect" title="Side effects">side effects</a>; or <a href="/wiki/Nocebo" title="Nocebo">nocebo effects</a> towards standard treatment), or the <a href="/wiki/Regression_toward_the_mean" title="Regression toward the mean">natural course of the condition or disease</a>. Alternative treatment is not the same as <a href="/wiki/Medical_research" title="Medical research">experimental treatment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a>, although both can be misused in ways that are alternative. Alternative or complementary medicine is dangerous because it may discourage people from getting the best possible treatment, and may lead to a false <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">understanding of the body</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">science</a>.\n</p><p>Alternative medicine is used by a significant number of people, though its popularity is often overstated. Large amounts of funding go to testing alternative medicine, with more than US$2.5 <a href="/wiki/Billion" title="Billion">billion</a> spent by the United States government alone. Almost none show any effect beyond that of <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">false treatment</a>, and most studies showing any effect have been statistical flukes. Alternative medicine is a highly profitable industry, with a strong lobby. This fact is often overlooked by media or intentionally kept hidden, with alternative practice being portrayed positively when compared to <a href="/wiki/Big_Pharma_conspiracy_theory" title="Big Pharma conspiracy theory">"big pharma"</a>. The lobby has successfully pushed for alternative therapies to be subject to far less regulation than conventional medicine. Alternative therapies may even be allowed to promote use when there is demonstrably no effect, only a tradition of use. Regulation and licensing of alternative medicine and <a href="/wiki/Health_care_provider" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care provider">health care providers</a> varies between and within countries. Despite laws making it illegal to market or promote alternative therapies for use in cancer treatment, many practitioners promote them. Alternative medicine is criticized for taking advantage of the weakest members of society. For example, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Institutes_of_Health" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Institutes of Health">United States National Institutes of Health</a> department studying alternative medicine, currently named <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Integrative_Health" title="National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health">National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health</a>, was established as the <i>Office of Alternative Medicine</i> and was renamed the <i>National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine</i> before obtaining its current name. Therapies are often <a href="/wiki/Framing_(psychology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Framing (psychology)">framed</a> as "natural" or "holistic", in apparent opposition to conventional medicine which is "artificial" and "narrow in scope", statements which are intentionally misleading. When used together with functional medical treatment, alternative therapies do not "complement" (improve the effect of, or mitigate the side effects of) treatment. Significant <a href="/wiki/Drug_interaction" title="Drug interaction">drug interactions</a> caused by alternative therapies may instead negatively impact functional treatment, making it less effective, notably in <a href="/wiki/Treatment_of_cancer" title="Treatment of cancer">cancer</a>.\n</p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Category:Alternative_medical_diagnostic_methods" title="Category:Alternative medical diagnostic methods">Alternative diagnoses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Alternative_medical_treatments" title="Category:Alternative medical treatments">treatments</a> are not part of <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, or of science-based curricula in <a href="/wiki/Medical_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical schools">medical schools</a>, nor are they used in any practice based on scientific knowledge or experience. Alternative therapies are often based on <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious belief</a>, tradition, <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>, belief in <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> energies, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">errors in reasoning</a>, propaganda, fraud, or lies. Alternative medicine is based on misleading statements, <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antiscience" title="Antiscience">antiscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a>, and poor <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific methodology</a>. Promoting alternative medicine has been called dangerous and <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">unethical</a>. Testing alternative medicine that has no scientific basis has been called a waste of scarce research resources. Critics state that "there is really no such thing as alternative medicine, just medicine that works and medicine that doesn\'t", that the very idea of "alternative" treatments is <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">paradoxical</a>, as any treatment proven to work is by definition "medicine". </p><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label <span class="toctext">Medical education</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Efficacy"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Efficacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Mechanism_of_action"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Mechanism of action</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Placebo_effect"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Placebo effect</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Use_and_regulation"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Use and regulation</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a class="toctext">Marketing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-27"><a href="#Social_factors"><span class="tocnumber">6.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Social factors</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Prevalence_of_use"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Prevalence of use</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-29"><a href="#In_the_US"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">In the US</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-30"><a href="#Prevalence_of_use_of_specific_therapies"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Prevalence of use of specific therapies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-31"><a href="#In_palliative_care"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">In palliative care</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 href="#Risks_and_problems"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Risks and problems</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Negative_outcomes"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Adequacy of regulation and CAM safety</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-36"><a href="#Interactions_with_conventional_pharmaceuticals"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Interactions with conventional pharmaceuticals</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-37"><a href="#Side-effects"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Side-effects</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-38"><a href="#Treatment_delay"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Treatment delay</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-39"><a href="#Unconventional_cancer_&quot;cures&quot;"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Unconventional cancer "cures"</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Rejection_of_science"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Rejection of science</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Conflicts_of_interest"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Conflicts of interest</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#World_Health_Organization"><span class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">World Health Organization</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-49"><a href="#Journals"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Journals</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-51"><a href="#Criticism"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Criticism</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_terminology">Definitions and terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions and terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_medicine">Alternative medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Alternative medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>Alternative medicine</b> is defined loosely as a set of products, practices, and theories that are believed or perceived by their users to have the healing effects of <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NSFquote_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFquote-2">&#91;n 1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IOMquote_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOMquote-4">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup> but whose <a href="/wiki/Efficacy" title="Efficacy">effectiveness</a> has not been clearly established using <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific methods</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NSFquote_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFquote-2">&#91;n 1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Angellquote_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angellquote-6">&#91;n 3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IGPIAMAYP_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGPIAMAYP-9">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NP_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NP-10">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> or whose theory and practice is not part of <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-IOMquote_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOMquote-4">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harrisonquote_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisonquote-12">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WebMD2014quote_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WebMD2014quote-14">&#91;n 5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH2quote_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH2quote-16">&#91;n 6&#93;</a></sup> or whose theories or practices are directly contradicted by <a href="/wiki/Scientific_evidence" title="Scientific evidence">scientific evidence</a> or <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific principles</a> used in biomedicine.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMCER_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMCER-17">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> "Biomedicine" or "medicine" is that part of <a href="/wiki/Medical_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical science">medical science</a> that applies principles of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">biophysics</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural sciences</a> to <a href="/wiki/Clinical_practice" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical practice">clinical practice</a>, using scientific methods to establish the effectiveness of that practice. Unlike medicine,<sup id="cite_ref-Harrisonquote_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisonquote-12">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup> an alternative product or practice does not originate from using scientific methods, but may instead be based on <a href="/wiki/Testimonial" title="Testimonial">hearsay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, tradition, <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>, belief in <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> energies, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">errors in reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a>, or other unscientific sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Angellquote_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angellquote-6">&#91;n 3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <i>General Guidelines for Methodologies on Research and Evaluation of Traditional Medicine</i>, published in 2000 by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO), complementary and alternative medicine were defined as a broad set of health care practices that are not part of that country\'s own tradition and are not integrated into the dominant health care system.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_Trad_Med_Defs_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Trad_Med_Defs-18">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWHO2000_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWHO2000-19">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The expression also refers to a diverse range of related and unrelated products, practices, and theories ranging from biologically plausible practices and products and practices with some evidence, to practices and theories that are directly contradicted by basic science or clear evidence, and products that have been conclusively proven to be ineffective or even toxic and harmful.<sup id="cite_ref-IOMquote_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOMquote-4">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CAM_Sointu_Nissen_Eisenberg_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAM_Sointu_Nissen_Eisenberg-20">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DaguptaHStabler2011_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaguptaHStabler2011-21">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The terms <i>alternative medicine</i>, <i>complementary medicine</i>, <i>integrative medicine,</i> <i>holistic medicine</i>, <i>natural medicine</i>, <i>unorthodox medicine</i>, <i>fringe medicine</i>, <i>unconventional medicine</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/New_age" class="mw-redirect" title="New age">new age</a> medicine</i> are used interchangeably as having the same meaning and are almost synonymous in most contexts.<sup id="cite_ref-Shapiro_08_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shapiro_08-22">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bombardieri2000_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bombardieri2000-23">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shuval2012_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shuval2012-24">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Freedman2011_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman2011-25">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcia.angell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Marcia.angell.jpg/220px-Marcia.angell.jpg" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/06/Marcia.angell.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="307" data-file-height="418" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcia.angell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marcia_Angell" title="Marcia Angell">Marcia Angell</a>: "There cannot be two kinds of medicine&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 conventional and alternative".</div></div></div>\n<p>The meaning of the term "alternative" in the expression "alternative medicine", is not that it is an effective alternative to <a href="/wiki/Medical_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical science">medical science</a>, although some alternative medicine promoters may use the loose terminology to give the appearance of effectiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kolata_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kolata-26">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Loose terminology may also be used to suggest meaning that a dichotomy exists when it does not, e.g., the use of the expressions "western medicine" and "eastern medicine" to suggest that the difference is a cultural difference between the Asiatic east and the European west, rather than that the difference is between <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a> and treatments that do not work.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complementary_or_integrative_medicine">Complementary or integrative medicine <span id="Complementary_medicine"></span><span id="Integrative_medicine"></span><span id="Integrated_medicine"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Complementary or integrative medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>Complementary medicine</b> (<b>CM</b>) or <b>integrative medicine</b> (<b>IM</b>) is the term used to describe the combined use of alternative medicine with functional medical treatment, in the <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> that it improves the effect of treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalReport_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalReport-28">&#91;n 7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ernst_1995_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_1995-29">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Joyce_1994_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joyce_1994-30">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BMJ_May_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ_May-31">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> However, significant <a href="/wiki/Drug_interaction" title="Drug interaction">drug interactions</a> caused by alternative therapies may instead negatively influence treatment, making treatments less effective, notably <a href="/wiki/Treatment_of_cancer" title="Treatment of cancer">cancer therapy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-32">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arye13_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arye13-33">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Both terms refer to use of alternative medical treatments alongside conventional medicine,<sup id="cite_ref-NCI_PDQ_CAM_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCI_PDQ_CAM-34">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Borkan2012_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borkan2012-35">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-whatiscam_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatiscam-36">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> an example of which is use of <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a> (sticking needles in the body to influence the flow of a supernatural energy), along with using science-based medicine, in the belief that the acupuncture increases the effectiveness or "complements" the science-based medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-whatiscam_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatiscam-36">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><b>CAM</b> is an abbreviation of the phrase <i>complementary and alternative medicine</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ernstinterview_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ernstinterview-37">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CassilethDeng2004_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CassilethDeng2004-38">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It has also been called sCAM or SCAM with the addition of "so-called" or "supplements".<sup id="cite_ref-skepdic.com_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skepdic.com-39">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tyreman_209\xe2\x80\x9317_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyreman_209\xe2\x80\x9317-40">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><span id="Allopathic_medicine"></span><span id="Allopathy"></span>\n</p>\n<h3><span id=".22Allopathic_medicine.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Allopathic_medicine&quot;">"Allopathic medicine"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: &quot;Allopathic medicine&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allopathic_medicine" title="Allopathic medicine">Allopathic medicine</a></div>\n<p><i>Allopathic medicine</i> or <i>allopathy</i> is an expression commonly used by <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopaths</a> and proponents of other forms of alternative medicine to refer to <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">mainstream medicine</a>. It was used to describe the traditional European practice of <a href="/wiki/Heroic_medicine" title="Heroic medicine">heroic medicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ConsumerHealth9th_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ConsumerHealth9th-41">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> but later continued to be used to describe anything that was not homeopathy.<sup id="cite_ref-ConsumerHealth9th_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ConsumerHealth9th-41">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Allopathy refers to the use of pharmacologically active <a href="/wiki/Active_ingredient" title="Active ingredient">agents</a> or <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">physical interventions</a> to treat or suppress <a href="/wiki/Symptoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Symptoms">symptoms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiology" title="Pathophysiology">pathophysiologic</a> processes of diseases or conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FREEDICT_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FREEDICT-42">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>  The German version of the word, <em>allopathisch</em>, was coined in 1810 by the creator of homeopathy, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hahnemann" title="Samuel Hahnemann">Samuel Hahnemann</a> (1755\xe2\x80\x931843).<sup id="cite_ref-Whorton2003_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whorton2003-43">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The word was coined from <em>allo-</em> (different) and <em>-pathic</em> (relating to a disease or to a method of treatment).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In alternative medicine circles the expression "allopathic medicine" is still used to refer to "the broad category of medical practice that is sometimes called Western medicine, <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>, or modern medicine" (see the article on <a href="/wiki/Scientific_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific medicine">scientific medicine</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-45">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Use of the term remains common among homeopaths and has spread to other alternative medicine practices. The meaning implied by the label has never been accepted by conventional medicine and is considered pejorative.<sup id="cite_ref-Atwood2004_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atwood2004-46">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> More recently, some sources have used the term "allopathic", particularly American sources wishing to distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_MD_and_DO_in_the_United_States" title="Comparison of MD and DO in the United States">Doctors of Medicine (MD) and Doctors of Osteopathic Medicine (DO) in the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whorton2003_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whorton2003-43">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NRMP_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRMP-47">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> William Jarvis, an expert on alternative medicine and public health,<sup id="cite_ref-Closer_to_Truth_Participants_&#124;PBS_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Closer_to_Truth_Participants_|PBS-48">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> states that "although many modern therapies can be construed to conform to an allopathic rationale (e.g., using a laxative to relieve constipation), standard medicine has never paid allegiance to an allopathic principle" and that the label "allopath" was from the start "considered highly derisive by regular medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-Jarvis_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jarvis-49">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many conventional medical treatments do not fit the nominal definition of <i>allopathy</i>, as they seek to prevent illness, or remove its cause.<sup id="cite_ref-Berkenwald1998_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berkenwald1998-50">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Federspil2003_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Federspil2003-51">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CAM">CAM</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: CAM">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>CAM is an abbreviation of complementary and alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-ernstinterview_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ernstinterview-37">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CassilethDeng2004_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CassilethDeng2004-38">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It has also been called sCAM or SCAM with the addition of "so-called" or "supplements".<sup id="cite_ref-skepdic.com_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skepdic.com-39">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tyreman_209\xe2\x80\x9317_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyreman_209\xe2\x80\x9317-40">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The words balance and holism are often used, claiming to take into account a "whole" person, in contrast to the supposed reductionism of medicine. Due to its many names the field has been criticized for intense <a href="/wiki/Rebranding" title="Rebranding">rebranding</a> of what are essentially the same practices: as soon as one name is declared synonymous with <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a>, a new name is chosen.<sup id="cite_ref-Shapiro_08_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shapiro_08-22">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_medicine">Traditional medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Traditional medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">Traditional medicine</a></div>\n<p><b>Traditional medicine</b> refers to the pre-scientific practices of a certain culture, contrary to what is typically practiced in other cultures where medical science dominates.\n</p><p>"<a href="/wiki/Eastern_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern medicine">Eastern medicine</a>" typically refers to the traditional medicines of Asia where conventional bio-medicine penetrated much later.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holistic_medicine">Holistic medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Holistic medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small><i>(May 2018)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The words <i>balance</i> and <i>holism</i> are often used alongside <i>complementary</i> or <i>integrative</i> medicine, claiming to take into account a "whole" person, in contrast to the supposed reductionism of medicine. Due to its many names the field has been criticized for intense <a href="/wiki/Rebranding" title="Rebranding">rebranding</a> of what are essentially the same practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Shapiro_08_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shapiro_08-22">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_with_definition">Problems with definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Problems with definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Prominent members of the science<sup id="cite_ref-Offit2013_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offit2013-52">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dawkins2003a_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins2003a-53">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and biomedical science community<sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> say that it is not meaningful to define an alternative medicine that is separate from a conventional medicine, that the expressions "conventional medicine", "alternative medicine", "complementary medicine", "integrative medicine",  and "holistic medicine" do not refer to any medicine at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Offit2013_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offit2013-52">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dawkins2003a_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins2003a-53">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fontanarosa1998_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fontanarosa1998-54">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Others in both the biomedical and CAM communities say that CAM <i>cannot</i> be precisely defined because of the diversity of theories and practices it includes, and because the boundaries between CAM and biomedicine overlap, are porous, and change.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison&#39;s_Principles_of_Internal_Medicine20151chpt._14-E_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison&#39;s_Principles_of_Internal_Medicine20151chpt._14-E-11">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200514\xe2\x80\x9320_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200514\xe2\x80\x9320-55">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The expression "complementary and alternative medicine" (CAM) resists easy definition because the health systems and practices it refers to are diffuse, and its boundaries poorly defined.<sup id="cite_ref-CAM_Sointu_Nissen_Eisenberg_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAM_Sointu_Nissen_Eisenberg-20">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuggie2004&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidViDwi7s2VvMCpgPA20_20&#93;_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuggie2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidViDwi7s2VvMCpgPA20_20]-56">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;n 8&#93;</a></sup> Healthcare practices categorized as alternative may differ in their historical origin, theoretical basis, <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">diagnostic technique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Therapy" title="Therapy">therapeutic practice</a> and in their relationship to the medical mainstream.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESointu2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXC2UUP36bjACpgPA13_13&#93;_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESointu2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXC2UUP36bjACpgPA13_13]-59">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Some alternative therapies, including <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine (TCM)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a>, have antique origins in East or South Asia and are entirely alternative medical systems;<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> others, such as homeopathy and chiropractic, have origins in Europe or the United States and emerged in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGevitz1997&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZIqk31BXpksCpgPA603_603\xe2\x80\x9333&#93;_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGevitz1997[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZIqk31BXpksCpgPA603_603\xe2\x80\x9333]-61">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Some, such as <a href="/wiki/Osteopathy" title="Osteopathy">osteopathy</a> and chiropractic, employ manipulative physical methods of treatment; others, such as <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a>, are based on <a href="/wiki/Mind-body_intervention" class="mw-redirect" title="Mind-body intervention">mind-body interventions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005&#91;httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page18_18&#93;_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005[httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page18_18]-62">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Treatments considered alternative in one location may be considered conventional in another.<sup id="cite_ref-Nissen2013_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nissen2013-63">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Thus, chiropractic is not considered alternative in Denmark and likewise <a href="/wiki/Osteopathic_medicine_in_the_United_States" title="Osteopathic medicine in the United States">osteopathic medicine</a> is no longer thought of as an alternative therapy in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Nissen2013_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nissen2013-63">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Critics say the expression is deceptive because it implies there is an effective alternative to science-based medicine, and that <i>complementary</i> is deceptive because it implies that the treatment increases the effectiveness of (complements) science-based medicine, while alternative medicines that have been tested nearly always have no measurable positive effect compared to a <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Skep_Dic_comp_med_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skep_Dic_comp_med-64">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Novella2010_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novella2010-65">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gorski2010_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorski2010-66">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Different_types_of_definitions">Different types of definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Different types of definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>One common feature of all definitions of alternative medicine is its designation as "other than" conventional medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005&#91;httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page14_14&#93;_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005[httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page14_14]-67">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> For example, the widely referenced<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005&#91;httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page19_19&#93;_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005[httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page19_19]-68">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> descriptive definition of complementary and alternative medicine devised by the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine">US National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health (NCCIH)</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health (NIH)</a>, states that it is "a group of diverse medical and health care systems, practices, and products that are not generally considered part of conventional medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> For conventional medical practitioners, it does not necessarily follow that either it or its practitioners would no longer be considered alternative.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;n 9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some definitions seek to specify alternative medicine in terms of its social and political marginality to mainstream healthcare.<sup id="cite_ref-Saks1992_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saks1992-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> This can refer to the lack of support that alternative therapies receive from the medical establishment and related bodies regarding access to <a href="/wiki/Medical_research#Funding" title="Medical research">research funding</a>, sympathetic coverage in the <a href="/wiki/Medical_literature" title="Medical literature">medical press</a>, or inclusion in the standard <a href="/wiki/Medical_education" title="Medical education">medical curriculum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Saks1992_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saks1992-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, the <a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Association" title="British Medical Association">British Medical Association (BMA)</a>, one among many professional organizations who have attempted to define alternative medicine, stated that it<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;n 10&#93;</a></sup> referred to "...those forms of treatment which are not widely used by the conventional healthcare professions, and the skills of which are not taught as part of the undergraduate curriculum of conventional medical and paramedical healthcare courses".<sup id="cite_ref-BMA_1993_1st_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMA_1993_1st-74">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In a US context, an influential definition coined in 1993 by the Harvard-based physician,<sup id="cite_ref-Artus2007_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Artus2007-76">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> David M. Eisenberg,<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg_Bio_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg_Bio-77">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> characterized alternative medicine "as interventions neither taught widely in medical schools nor generally available in US hospitals".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> These descriptive definitions are inadequate in the present-day when some conventional doctors offer alternative medical treatments and CAM introductory courses or modules can be offered as part of standard undergraduate medical training;<sup id="cite_ref-HOL_Kopelman_Wieland_Astin_Pelletier_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOL_Kopelman_Wieland_Astin_Pelletier-79">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> alternative medicine is taught in more than 50 per cent of <a href="/wiki/Medical_school_in_the_United_States" title="Medical school in the United States">US medical schools</a> and increasingly <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance_in_the_United_States" title="Health insurance in the United States">US health insurers</a> are willing to provide reimbursement for CAM therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005&#91;httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page17_17&#93;,_&#91;httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page196_196\xe2\x80\x93252&#93;_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005[httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page17_17],_[httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page196_196\xe2\x80\x93252]-80">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In 1999, 7.7% of US hospitals reported using some form of CAM therapy; this proportion had risen to 37.7% by 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Salomonsen_AHA_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salomonsen_AHA-81">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An expert panel at a conference hosted in 1995 by the US Office for Alternative Medicine (OAM),<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-82">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;n 11&#93;</a></sup> devised a <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_definition" title="Theoretical definition">theoretical definition</a><sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-82">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> of alternative medicine as "a broad domain of healing resources&#160;... other than those intrinsic to the politically dominant health system of a particular society or culture in a given historical period".<sup id="cite_ref-CAM_RMC1997_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAM_RMC1997-85">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> This definition has been widely adopted by CAM researchers,<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-82">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> cited by official government bodies such as the UK Department of Health,<sup id="cite_ref-UKDoH_CAM_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKDoH_CAM-86">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> attributed as the definition used by the <a href="/wiki/Cochrane_Collaboration" class="mw-redirect" title="Cochrane Collaboration">Cochrane Collaboration</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ZollChanCoulRobeAdamArtu_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZollChanCoulRobeAdamArtu-87">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and, with some modification,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (June 2015)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Alternative_medicine#Dubious" title="Talk:Alternative medicine">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup> was preferred in the 2005 consensus report of the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute of Medicine">US Institute of Medicine</a>, <i>Complementary and Alternative Medicine in the United States</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-IOMquote_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOMquote-4">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 1995 OAM conference definition, an expansion of Eisenberg\'s 1993 formulation, is silent regarding questions of the medical effectiveness of alternative therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-Hufford2004_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hufford2004-88">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Its proponents hold that it thus avoids <a href="/wiki/Relativism" title="Relativism">relativism</a> about differing forms of medical knowledge and, while it is an essentially political definition, this should not imply that the dominance of mainstream <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedicine</a> is solely due to political forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Hufford2004_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hufford2004-88">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> According to this definition, alternative and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">mainstream medicine</a> can only be differentiated with reference to what is "intrinsic to the politically dominant health system of a particular society of culture".<sup id="cite_ref-Kopelman2004_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kopelman2004-89">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> However, there is neither a reliable method to distinguish between cultures and <a href="/wiki/Subculture" title="Subculture">subcultures</a>, nor to attribute them as dominant or subordinate, nor any accepted criteria to determine the dominance of a cultural entity.<sup id="cite_ref-Kopelman2004_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kopelman2004-89">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> If the culture of a politically dominant healthcare system is held to be equivalent to the perspectives of those charged with the medical management of leading healthcare institutions and programs, the definition fails to recognize the potential for division either within such an elite or between a healthcare elite and the wider population.<sup id="cite_ref-Kopelman2004_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kopelman2004-89">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">Normative</a> definitions distinguish alternative medicine from the biomedical mainstream in its provision of therapies that are unproven, unvalidated, or ineffective and support of theories with no recognized scientific basis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005&#91;httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page17_17&#93;_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005[httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page17_17]-90">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> These definitions characterize practices as constituting alternative medicine when, used independently or in place of <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>, they are put forward as having the healing effects of medicine, but are not based on <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> gathered with the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCI_PDQ_CAM_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCI_PDQ_CAM-34">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Borkan2012_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borkan2012-35">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kong2005_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kong2005-91">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Exemplifying this perspective, a 1998 editorial co-authored by <a href="/wiki/Marcia_Angell" title="Marcia Angell">Marcia Angell</a>, a former editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_England_Journal_of_Medicine" title="The New England Journal of Medicine">The New England Journal of Medicine</a></i>, argued that:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>It is time for the scientific community to stop giving alternative medicine a free ride. There cannot be two kinds of medicine&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 conventional and alternative. There is only medicine that has been adequately tested and medicine that has not, medicine that works and medicine that may or may not work. Once a treatment has been tested rigorously, it no longer matters whether it was considered alternative at the outset. If it is found to be reasonably safe and effective, it will be accepted. But assertions, speculation, and testimonials do not substitute for evidence. Alternative treatments should be subjected to scientific testing no less rigorous than that required for conventional treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-Angell1998_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angell1998-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>This line of division has been subject to criticism, however, as not all forms of standard medical practice have adequately demonstrated evidence of benefit,<sup id="cite_ref-Harrisonquote_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisonquote-12">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ezzo2001_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ezzo2001-92">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200517\xe2\x80\x9318_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report200517\xe2\x80\x9318-93">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and it is also unlikely in most instances that conventional therapies, if proven to be ineffective, would ever be classified as CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-82">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Similarly, the public information website maintained by the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_and_Medical_Research_Council" title="National Health and Medical Research Council">National Health and Medical Research Council</a> (NHMRC) of the Commonwealth of Australia uses the acronym "CAM" for a wide range of health care practices, therapies, procedures and devices not within the domain of conventional medicine. In the Australian context this is stated to include acupuncture; aromatherapy; chiropractic; homeopathy; massage; meditation and relaxation therapies; naturopathy; osteopathy; reflexology, traditional Chinese medicine; and the use of vitamin supplements.<sup id="cite_ref-NHMRC_CAM_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHMRC_CAM-94">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Board_of_Health_(Denmark)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Board of Health (Denmark)">Danish National Board of Health\'s</a> "Council for Alternative Medicine" (Sundhedsstyrelsens R\xc3\xa5d for Alternativ Behandling (SRAB)), an independent institution under the National Board of Health (Danish: <i>Sundhedsstyrelsen</i>), uses the term "alternative medicine" for:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Treatments performed by therapists that are not authorized healthcare professionals.</li>\n<li>Treatments performed by authorized healthcare professionals, but those based on methods otherwise used mainly outside the healthcare system. People without a healthcare authorisation are [also] allowed to perform the treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-VIFAB_definition_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VIFAB_definition-95">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Proponents of an <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-base for medicine</a><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;n 12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nuffield_Trust_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuffield_Trust-98">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Belsey2009_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belsey2009-99">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nuffield2_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuffield2-100">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bardsley2011_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardsley2011-101">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> such as the Cochrane Collaboration (founded in 1993 and from 2011 providing input for WHO resolutions) take a position that <i>all</i> systematic reviews of treatments, whether "mainstream" or "alternative", ought to be held to the current standards of scientific method.<sup id="cite_ref-UM_CAM_Field_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UM_CAM_Field-102">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In a study titled <i>Development and classification of an operational definition of complementary and alternative medicine for the Cochrane Collaboration</i> (2011) it was proposed that indicators that a therapy is accepted include government licensing of practitioners, coverage by <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a>, statements of approval by government agencies, and recommendation as part of a practice guideline; and that if something is currently a standard, accepted therapy, then it is not likely to be widely considered as CAM.<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_82-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-82">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_forms_of_alternative_medicine" title="List of forms of alternative medicine">List of forms of alternative medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pseudomedicine#List_of_fields_characterized_as_pseudomedicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudomedicine">Pseudomedicine \xc2\xa7&#160;List of fields characterized as pseudomedicine</a></div>\n<p>Alternative medicine consists of a wide range of health care practices, products, and therapies. The shared feature is a claim to heal that is not based on the scientific method. Alternative medicine practices are diverse in their foundations and methodologies.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Alternative medicine practices may be classified by their cultural origins or by the types of beliefs upon which they are based.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMCER_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMCER-17">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Methods may incorporate or be based on traditional medicinal practices of a particular culture, folk knowledge, superstition,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Connor1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJXFPb88KLZQCpgPA2_2&#93;_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Connor1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJXFPb88KLZQCpgPA2_2]-103">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> spiritual beliefs, belief in <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> energies (<a href="/wiki/Antiscience" title="Antiscience">antiscience</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">errors in reasoning</a>, propaganda, fraud, new or different concepts of health and disease, and any bases other than being proven by scientific methods.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hines_Sampson_Coulter_Sagan-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMCER_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMCER-17">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Different cultures may have their own unique traditional or belief based practices developed recently or over thousands of years, and specific practices or entire systems of practices.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unscientific_belief_systems">Unscientific belief systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Unscientific belief systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Alternative medicine, such as using <a href="/wiki/Naturopathy" title="Naturopathy">naturopathy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a> in place of <a href="/wiki/Conventional_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Conventional medicine">conventional medicine</a>, is based on belief systems not grounded in science.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>\n</th>\n<th>Proposed mechanism\n</th>\n<th>Issues\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Naturopathy" title="Naturopathy">Naturopathy</a>\n</th>\n<td>Naturopathic medicine is based on a belief that the body heals itself using a supernatural <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vital energy</a> that guides bodily processes.<sup id="cite_ref-SarrisWardle2010_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SarrisWardle2010-104">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>In conflict with the paradigm of <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jagtenberg2006_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jagtenberg2006-105">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Many naturopaths have opposed <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2001_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2001-106">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> and "scientific evidence does not support claims that naturopathic medicine can cure <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> or any other disease".<sup id="cite_ref-ACS_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACS-107">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">Homeopathy</a>\n</th>\n<td>A belief that a substance that causes the symptoms of a disease in healthy people cures similar symptoms in sick people.<sup id="cite_ref-Hahnemannquote_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hahnemannquote-109">&#91;n 13&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Developed before knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, or of basic chemistry, which shows that repeated dilution as practiced in homeopathy produces only water, and that homeopathy is not scientifically valid.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2002_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2002-110">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HCReport2010_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HCReport2010-111">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-shang_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shang-112">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH_homeopathy_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH_homeopathy-113">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supplements">Supplements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Supplements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small><i>(May 2018)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_person_discovering_that_they_have_been_transformed_into_se_Wellcome_V0011124.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/A_person_discovering_that_they_have_been_transformed_into_se_Wellcome_V0011124.jpg/300px-A_person_discovering_that_they_have_been_transformed_into_se_Wellcome_V0011124.jpg"
