srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/A_person_discovering_that_they_have_been_transformed_into_se_Wellcome_V0011124.jpg/450px-A_person_discovering_that_they_have_been_transformed_into_se_Wellcome_V0011124.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/A_person_discovering_that_they_have_been_transformed_into_se_Wellcome_V0011124.jpg/600px-A_person_discovering_that_they_have_been_transformed_into_se_Wellcome_V0011124.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2604" data-file-height="2922" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_person_discovering_that_they_have_been_transformed_into_se_Wellcome_V0011124.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<i>They told me if I took 1000 pills at night I should be quite another thing in the morning</i>", an early 19th-century satire on <a href="/wiki/Morison%27s_Vegetable_Pills" class="mw-redirect" title="Morison&#39;s Vegetable Pills">Morison\'s Vegetable Pills</a>, an alternative medicine supplement.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_ethnic_systems">Traditional ethnic systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Traditional ethnic systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_(18).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG/150px-Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG/225px-Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG/300px-Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_%2818%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Xi%27an_traditionnal_medecine_market_(18).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ready-to-drink <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> mixture</div></div></div>\n<p>Alternative medical systems may be based on <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a> practices, such as <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> (TCM), <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a> in India, or practices of other cultures around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Some useful applications of traditional medicines have been researched and accepted within ordinary medicine, however the underlying belief systems are seldom scientific and are not accepted.\n</p><p>Traditional medicine is considered alternative when it is used outside its home region; or when it is used together with or instead of known functional treatment; or when it can be reasonably expected that the patient or practitioner knows or should know that it will not work \xe2\x80\x93 such as knowing that the practice is based on superstition.\n</p><p>Since ancient times, in many parts of the world a number of herbs reputed to possess abortifacient properties have been used in <a href="/wiki/Folk_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk medicine">folk medicine</a>. Among these are: <a href="/wiki/Tansy" title="Tansy">tansy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mentha_pulegium" title="Mentha pulegium">pennyroyal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_cohosh" class="mw-redirect" title="Black cohosh">black cohosh</a>, and the now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Silphium" title="Silphium">silphium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-riddle2_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riddle2-114">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>44\xe2\x80\x9347, 62\xe2\x80\x9363, 154\xe2\x80\x9355, 230\xe2\x80\x9331</span></sup> Historian of science <a href="/wiki/Ann_Hibner_Koblitz" title="Ann Hibner Koblitz">Ann Hibner Koblitz</a> has written of the probable <a href="/wiki/Protoscience" title="Protoscience">protoscientific</a> origins of this folk knowledge in observation of farm animals.  Women who knew that grazing on certain plants would cause an animal to abort (with negative economic consequences for the farm) would be likely to try out those plants on themselves in order to avoid an unwanted pregnancy.<sup id="cite_ref-ahk_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ahk-115">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>120</span></sup>\n</p><p>However, modern users of these plants often lack knowledge of the proper preparation and dosage.  The historian of medicine <a href="/wiki/John_M._Riddle" title="John M. Riddle">John Riddle</a> has spoken of the "broken chain of knowledge" caused by urbanization and modernization,<sup id="cite_ref-riddle2_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riddle2-114">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>167\xe2\x80\x93205</span></sup> and Koblitz has written that "folk knowledge about effective contraception techniques often disappears over time or becomes inextricably mixed with useless or harmful practices."<sup id="cite_ref-ahk_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ahk-115">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>vii</span></sup>  The ill-informed or indiscriminant use of herbs as abortifacients can cause serious and even lethal side-effects.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>\n</th>\n<th>Claims\n</th>\n<th>Issues\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Traditional Chinese medicine</a>\n</td>\n<td>Traditional practices and beliefs from China, together with modifications made by the Communist party make up TCM. Common practices include herbal medicine, <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a> (insertion of needles in the body at specified points), massage (Tui na), exercise (<a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">qigong</a>), and dietary therapy.\n</td>\n<td>The practices are based on belief in a supernatural energy called <a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a>, considerations of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astrology" title="Chinese astrology">Chinese Astrology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Numerology#Chinese_numerology" title="Numerology">Chinese numerology</a>, traditional use of herbs and other substances found in China&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 a belief that the tongue contains a map of the body that reflects changes in the body, and an incorrect model of the anatomy and physiology of internal organs.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BeyersteinSampson1996_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeyersteinSampson1996-118">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LuNeedham1980_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LuNeedham1980-119">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maciocia1995_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maciocia1995-120">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Matuk2006_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matuk2006-121">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Deshpande1987_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deshpande1987-122">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a>\n</td>\n<td>Traditional medicine of India. Ayurveda believes in the existence of three elemental substances, the <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda#Tridosha_system" title="Ayurveda">doshas</a> (called Vata, Pitta and Kapha), and states that a balance of the doshas results in health, while imbalance results in disease. Such disease-inducing imbalances can be adjusted and balanced using traditional herbs, minerals and heavy metals. Ayurveda stresses the use of plant-based medicines and treatments, with some animal products, and added minerals, including <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper_sulfate" title="Copper sulfate">copper sulfate</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.\n</td>\n<td>Safety concerns have been raised about Ayurveda, with two U.S. studies finding about 20 percent of Ayurvedic Indian-manufactured <a href="/wiki/Patent_medicine" title="Patent medicine">patent medicines</a> contained toxic levels of heavy metals such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>. A 2015 study of users in the United States also found elevated blood lead levels in 40 percent of those tested.  Other concerns include the use of herbs containing toxic compounds and the lack of quality control in Ayurvedic facilities. Incidents of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy metal poisoning">heavy metal poisoning</a> have been attributed to the use of these compounds in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWujastyk2003xviii_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWujastyk2003xviii-123">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMishra20048_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMishra20048-124">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DaguptaHStabler2011_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaguptaHStabler2011-21">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Valiathan2006_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valiathan2006-125">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CDC_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-126">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Saper2008_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saper2008-127">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supernatural_energies">Supernatural energies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Supernatural energies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Bases of belief may include belief in existence of supernatural energies undetected by the science of physics, as in biofields, or in belief in properties of the energies of physics that are inconsistent with the laws of physics, as in energy medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>\n</th>\n<th>Claims\n</th>\n<th>Issues\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Energy_medicine" title="Energy medicine">Biofield therapy</a>\n</td>\n<td>Intended to influence energy fields that, it is purported, surround and penetrate the body.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Writers such as noted astrophysicist and advocate of skeptical thinking (<a href="/wiki/Scientific_skepticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific skepticism">Scientific skepticism</a>) <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> (1934\xe2\x80\x931996) have described the lack of empirical evidence to support the existence of the putative energy fields on which these therapies are predicated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESagan1996_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESagan1996-130">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Bioelectromagnetism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioelectromagnetism">Bioelectromagnetic</a> therapy\n</td>\n<td>Use verifiable electromagnetic fields, such as pulsed fields, alternating-current, or direct-current fields in an unconventional manner.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Asserts that magnets can be used to defy the laws of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> to influence health and disease.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Chiropractic" title="Chiropractic">Chiropractic</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Spinal_manipulation" title="Spinal manipulation">Spinal manipulation</a> aims to treat "<a href="/wiki/Vertebral_subluxation" title="Vertebral subluxation">vertebral subluxations</a>" which are claimed to put pressure on nerves.\n</td>\n<td>Chiropractic was developed in the belief that manipulating the spine affects the flow of a supernatural <a href="/wiki/Vital_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Vital energy">vital energy</a> and thereby affects health and disease. Vertebral subluxation is a pseudoscientific concept and has not been proven to exist.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Reiki\n</td>\n<td>Practitioners place their palms on the patient near <a href="/wiki/Chakras" class="mw-redirect" title="Chakras">Chakras</a> that they believe are centers of supernatural energies in the belief that these supernatural energies can transfer from the practitioner\'s palms to heal the patient.\n</td>\n<td>Lacks credible scientific evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-rosa_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosa-131">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Acupuncture1-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Acupuncture1-1.jpg/220px-Acupuncture1-1.jpg" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Acupuncture1-1.jpg/330px-Acupuncture1-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Acupuncture1-1.jpg/440px-Acupuncture1-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Acupuncture1-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">Acupuncture</a> involves insertion of needles in the body.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holistic_therapy">Holistic therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Holistic therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>\n</th>\n<th>Claims\n</th>\n<th>Issues\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Mind-body_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Mind-body medicine">Mind-body medicine</a>\n</td>\n<td>The mind can affect "bodily functions and symptoms" and there is an interconnection between the mind, body, and spirit.\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Herbal_remedies_and_other_substances_used">Herbal remedies and other substances used</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Herbal remedies and other substances used">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Phytotherapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Phytotherapy">Phytotherapy</a></div><p>\nSubstance based practices use substances found in nature such as herbs, foods, non-vitamin supplements and megavitamins, animal and fungal products, and minerals, including use of these products in traditional medical practices that may also incorporate other methods.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH1_69-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abdulla1999_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdulla1999-132">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Examples include healing claims for nonvitamin supplements, <a href="/wiki/Fish_oil" title="Fish oil">fish oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acid" title="Omega-3 fatty acid">Omega-3 fatty acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucosamine" title="Glucosamine">glucosamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echinacea" title="Echinacea">echinacea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flaxseed_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Flaxseed oil">flaxseed oil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ginseng" title="Ginseng">ginseng</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIHSurvey_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIHSurvey-134">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herbal_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbal medicine">Herbal medicine</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Phytotherapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Phytotherapy">phytotherapy</a>, includes not just the use of plant products, but may also include the use of animal and mineral products.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdulla1999_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdulla1999-132">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> It is among the most commercially successful branches of alternative medicine, and includes the tablets, powders and elixirs that are sold as "nutritional supplements".<sup id="cite_ref-Abdulla1999_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdulla1999-132">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Only a very small percentage of these have been shown to have any efficacy, and there is little regulation as to standards and safety of their contents.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdulla1999_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdulla1999-132">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> This may include use of known toxic substances, such as use of the poison <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> in traditional Chinese medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIHSurvey_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIHSurvey-134">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg/220px-Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg/330px-Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg/440px-Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2936" data-file-height="2202" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chiropractic_spinal_adjustment.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Chiropractor" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiropractor">chiropractor</a> "<a href="/wiki/Spinal_adjustment" title="Spinal adjustment">adjusting</a>" the spine.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span id="Religion.2C_faith_healing.2C_and_prayer"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion,_faith_healing,_and_prayer">Religion, faith healing, and prayer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Religion, faith healing, and prayer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>\n</th>\n<th>Claims\n</th>\n<th>Issues\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Faith_healing" title="Faith healing">faith healing</a>\n</td>\n<td>There is a divine or spiritual intervention in healing.\n</td>\n<td>Lack of evidence for effectiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-Pearce_Simpson1998_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce_Simpson1998-135">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Unwanted outcomes, such as death and disability, "have occurred when faith healing was elected instead of medical care for serious injuries or illnesses".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> A 2001 double-blind study of 799 discharged coronary surgery patients found that "intercessory prayer had no significant effect on medical outcomes after hospitalization in a coronary care unit."<sup id="cite_ref-Aviles_et_al2001_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aviles_et_al2001-137">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">Shamanism</a>\n</td>\n<td>A practitioner can reach an <a href="/wiki/Altered_state_of_consciousness" title="Altered state of consciousness">altered states of consciousness</a> in order to encounter and interact with the spirit world or channel supernatural energies in the belief that they can heal.\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NCCIH_classification">NCCIH classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: NCCIH classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A US agency, National Center on Complementary and Integrative Health (<a href="/wiki/NCCIH" class="mw-redirect" title="NCCIH">NCCIH</a>), has created a classification system for branches of complementary and alternative medicine that divides them into five major groups. These groups have some overlap, and distinguish  two types of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> medicine: <i>veritable</i> which involves scientifically observable energy (including <a href="/wiki/Magnet_therapy" title="Magnet therapy">magnet therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colorpuncture" title="Colorpuncture">colorpuncture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Light_therapy" title="Light therapy">light therapy</a>) and <i>putative</i>, which invokes physically undetectable or unverifiable energy.<sup id="cite_ref-NCCIH_EMed_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH_EMed-138">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> None of these energies have any evidence to support that they effect the body in any positive or health promoting way.<sup id="cite_ref-ConsumerHealth9th_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ConsumerHealth9th-41">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li>Whole medical systems: Cut across more than one of the other groups; examples include traditional Chinese medicine, naturopathy, homeopathy, and ayurveda.</li>\n<li>Mind-body interventions: Explore the interconnection between the mind, body, and spirit, under the premise that they affect "bodily functions and symptoms". A connection between mind and body is conventional medical fact, and this classification does not include therapies with proven function such as <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">cognitive behavioral therapy</a>.</li>\n<li>"Biology"-based practices: Use substances found in nature such as herbs, foods, vitamins, and other natural substances. (Note that as used here, "biology" does <i>not</i> refer to the science of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, but is a usage newly coined by NCCIH in the primary source used for this article. "Biology-based" as coined by NCCIH may refer to chemicals from a nonbiological source, such as use of the poison <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> in traditional Chinese medicine, and to other nonbiological substances.)</li>\n<li>Manipulative and body-based practices: feature manipulation or movement of body parts, such as is done in bodywork, chiropractic, and osteopathic manipulation.</li>\n<li>Energy medicine: is a domain that deals with putative and verifiable energy fields:\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Energy_(spirituality)#Biofields" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy (spirituality)">Biofield</a> therapies are intended to influence energy fields that are purported to surround and penetrate the body. The existence of such energy fields have been disproven.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bioelectromagnetism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioelectromagnetism">Bioelectromagnetic</a>-based therapies use verifiable electromagnetic fields, such as pulsed fields, alternating-current, or direct-current fields in a non-scientific manner.</li></ul></li></ol>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_alternative_medicine" title="History of alternative medicine">History of alternative medicine</a></div>\n<p>The history of alternative medicine may refer to the history of a group of diverse medical practices that were collectively promoted as "alternative medicine" beginning in the 1970s, to the collection of individual histories of members of that group, or to the history of western medical practices that were labeled "irregular practices" by the western medical establishment.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-139">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-140">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-141">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RRCAM_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RRCAM-142">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> It includes the histories of <a href="/wiki/Complementary_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Complementary medicine">complementary medicine</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Integrative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrative medicine">integrative medicine</a>. Before the 1970s, western practitioners that were not part of the increasingly science-based medical establishment were referred to "irregular practitioners", and were dismissed by the medical establishment as unscientific and as practicing <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-139">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-140">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Until the 1970s, irregular practice became increasingly marginalized as <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a> and fraud, as western medicine increasingly incorporated scientific methods and discoveries, and had a corresponding increase in success of its treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-141">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In the 1970s, irregular practices were grouped with traditional practices of nonwestern cultures and with other unproven or disproven practices that were not part of biomedicine, with the entire group collectively marketed and promoted under the single expression "alternative medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-139">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-140">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-141">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FrontlineAF_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FrontlineAF-143">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Use of alternative medicine in the west began to rise following the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Counterculture movement">counterculture movement</a> of the 1960s, as part of the rising <a href="/wiki/New_age_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="New age movement">new age movement</a> of the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ENAB_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENAB-145">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> This was due to misleading <a href="/wiki/Mass_marketing" title="Mass marketing">mass marketing</a> of "alternative medicine" being an effective "alternative" to biomedicine, changing social attitudes about not using <a href="/wiki/Chemicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemicals">chemicals</a> and challenging <a href="/wiki/The_establishment" class="mw-redirect" title="The establishment">the establishment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> of any kind, sensitivity to giving equal measure to beliefs and practices of other cultures (<a href="/wiki/Cultural_relativism" title="Cultural relativism">cultural relativism</a>), and growing frustration and desperation by patients about limitations and <a href="/wiki/Side_effect" title="Side effect">side effects</a> of <a href="/wiki/Science-based_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Science-based medicine">science-based medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHBHAMA_140-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHBHAMA-140">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCHAMA_141-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCHAMA-141">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RRCAM_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RRCAM-142">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FrontlineAF_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FrontlineAF-143">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ENAB_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENAB-145">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, in 1975, the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a>, which played the central role in fighting quackery in the United States, abolished its quackery committee and closed down its Department of Investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-QMHCHF_139-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QMHCHF-139">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>xxi</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> By the early to mid 1970s the expression "alternative medicine" came into widespread use, and the expression became mass marketed as a collection of "natural" and effective treatment "alternatives" to science-based biomedicine.<sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> By 1983, mass marketing of "alternative medicine" was so pervasive that the <a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="British Medical Journal">British Medical Journal</a> (BMJ) pointed to "an apparently endless stream of books, articles, and radio and television programmes urge on the public the virtues of (alternative medicine) treatments ranging from meditation to drilling a hole in the skull to let in more oxygen".<sup id="cite_ref-AMBMJ_146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMBMJ-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_education">Medical education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Medical education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Mainly as a result of reforms following the Flexner Report of 1910<sup id="cite_ref-Flexner_Report_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flexner_Report-149">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Medical_education" title="Medical education">medical education</a> in established <a href="/wiki/Medical_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical schools">medical schools</a> in the US has generally not included alternative medicine as a teaching topic.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;n 14&#93;</a></sup> Typically, their teaching is based on current practice and scientific knowledge about: anatomy, physiology, histology, embryology, neuroanatomy, pathology, pharmacology, microbiology and immunology.<sup id="cite_ref-Bianco_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bianco-152">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Medical schools\' teaching includes such topics as doctor-patient communication, ethics, the art of medicine,<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_Curriculum_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford_Curriculum-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> and engaging in complex clinical reasoning (medical decision-making).<sup id="cite_ref-Yale_Curriculum_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yale_Curriculum-154">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Writing in 2002, Snyderman and Weil remarked that by the early twentieth century the Flexner model had helped to create the 20th-century academic health center, in which education, research, and practice were inseparable. While this had much improved medical practice by defining with increasing certainty the <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiological" class="mw-redirect" title="Pathophysiological">pathophysiological</a> basis of disease, a single-minded focus on the pathophysiological had diverted much of mainstream American medicine from clinical conditions that were not well understood in mechanistic terms, and were not effectively treated by conventional therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-Snyderman2002_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyderman2002-155">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 2001 some form of CAM training was being offered by at least 75 out of 125 medical schools in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman2001_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berman2001-156">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Exceptionally, the School of Medicine of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland,_Baltimore" title="University of Maryland, Baltimore">University of Maryland, Baltimore</a> includes a research institute for integrative medicine (a member entity of the Cochrane Collaboration).<sup id="cite_ref-UM_CAM_Field_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UM_CAM_Field-102">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UM_CIM_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UM_CIM-157">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Medical schools are responsible for conferring medical degrees, but a physician typically may not legally practice medicine until licensed by the local government authority. Licensed <a href="/wiki/Physicians_in_the_United_States" title="Physicians in the United States">physicians in the US</a> who have attended one of the established medical schools there have usually graduated Doctor of Medicine (MD).<sup id="cite_ref-Medline_MD_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medline_MD-158">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> All states require that applicants for MD licensure be graduates of an approved medical school and complete the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Medical_Licensing_Examination" title="United States Medical Licensing Examination">United States Medical Licensing Exam (USMLE)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Medline_MD_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medline_MD-158">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficacy">Efficacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Efficacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There is a general scientific consensus that alternative therapies lack the requisite <a href="/wiki/Scientific_validation" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific validation">scientific validation</a>, and their <a href="/wiki/Efficacy" title="Efficacy">effectiveness</a> is either <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">unproved</a> or <a href="/wiki/Scientific_evidence" title="Scientific evidence">disproved</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATRAMM_7-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATRAMM-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kent1997_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kent1997-159">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goldrosen2004_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldrosen2004-160">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Many of the claims regarding the efficacy of alternative medicines are controversial, since research on them is frequently of low quality and methodologically flawed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005-161">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Publication_bias" title="Publication bias">Selective publication bias</a>, marked differences in product quality and standardisation, and some companies making unsubstantiated claims call into question the claims of efficacy of isolated examples where there is evidence for alternative therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-Sarris2012_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sarris2012-162">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Scientific Review of Alternative Medicine</i> points to confusions in the general population&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 a person may attribute symptomatic relief to an otherwise-ineffective therapy just because they are taking something (the placebo effect); the natural recovery from or the cyclical nature of an illness (the <a href="/wiki/Regression_fallacy" title="Regression fallacy">regression fallacy</a>) gets misattributed to an alternative medicine being taken; a person not diagnosed with science-based medicine may never originally have had a true illness diagnosed as an alternative disease category.<sup id="cite_ref-Alcock1999_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alcock1999-163">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a> characterized the evidence for many alternative techniques as weak, nonexistent, or negative<sup id="cite_ref-goodbadugly_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodbadugly-164">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> and in 2011 published his estimate that about 7.4% were based on "sound evidence", although he believes that may be an overestimate.<sup id="cite_ref-Ernst2011_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2011-165">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Ernst has concluded that 95% of the alternative treatments he and his team studied, including acupuncture, herbal medicine, homeopathy, and reflexology, are "statistically indistinguishable from placebo treatments", but he also believes there is something that conventional doctors can usefully learn from the chiropractors and homeopath: this is the therapeutic value of the placebo effect, one of the strangest phenomena in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist2011_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist2011-166">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HHH2008_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HHH2008-167">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2003, a project funded by the CDC identified 208 condition-treatment pairs, of which 58% had been studied by at least one <a href="/wiki/Randomized_controlled_trial" title="Randomized controlled trial">randomized controlled trial</a> (RCT), and 23% had been assessed with a <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Katz2003_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katz2003-168">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2005 book by a US <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute of Medicine">Institute of Medicine</a> panel, the number of RCTs focused on CAM has risen dramatically.\n</p><p>As of  2005<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Cochrane_Library" title="Cochrane Library">Cochrane Library</a> had 145 CAM-related Cochrane systematic reviews and 340 non-Cochrane systematic reviews. An analysis of the conclusions of only the 145 Cochrane reviews was done by two readers. In 83% of the cases, the readers agreed. In the 17% in which they disagreed, a third reader agreed with one of the initial readers to set a rating. These studies found that, for CAM, 38.4% concluded positive effect or possibly positive (12.4%), 4.8% concluded no effect, 0.7% concluded harmful effect, and 56.6% concluded insufficient evidence. An assessment of conventional treatments found that 41.3% concluded positive or possibly positive effect, 20% concluded no effect, 8.1% concluded net harmful effects, and 21.3% concluded insufficient evidence. However, the CAM review used the more developed 2004 Cochrane database, while the conventional review used the initial 1998 Cochrane database.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005&#91;httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page135_135\xe2\x80\x9336&#93;_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIOM_Report2005[httpbooksnapeduopenbookphprecord_id11182page135_135\xe2\x80\x9336]-169">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the same way as for conventional therapies, drugs, and interventions, it can be difficult to test the efficacy of alternative medicine in <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trials</a>. In instances where an established, effective, treatment for a condition is already available, the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Declaration" class="mw-redirect" title="Helsinki Declaration">Helsinki Declaration</a> states that withholding such treatment is unethical in most circumstances. Use of standard-of-care treatment in addition to an alternative technique being tested may produce confounded or difficult-to-interpret results.<sup id="cite_ref-Bodeker2002_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bodeker2002-170">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cancer researcher <a href="/wiki/Andrew_J._Vickers" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew J. Vickers">Andrew J. Vickers</a> has stated:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Contrary to much popular and scientific writing, many <a href="/wiki/Alternative_cancer_treatments" title="Alternative cancer treatments">alternative cancer treatments</a> have been investigated in good-quality clinical trials, and they have been shown to be ineffective. The label "unproven" is inappropriate for such therapies; it is time to assert that many alternative cancer therapies have been "disproven".<sup id="cite_ref-Vickers_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vickers-171">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism_of_action">Mechanism of action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mechanism of action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Placebo_effect">Placebo effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Placebo effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A research methods expert and author of <i>Snake Oil Science</i>, <a href="/wiki/R._Barker_Bausell" title="R. Barker Bausell">R. Barker Bausell</a>, has stated that "it\'s become politically correct to investigate nonsense."<sup id="cite_ref-$2.5_billion_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-$2.5_billion-172">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> There are concerns that just having NIH support is being used to give unfounded "legitimacy to treatments that are not legitimate."<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2009_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2009-173">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Use of placebos to achieve a <a href="/wiki/Placebo_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Placebo effect">placebo effect</a> in integrative medicine has been criticized as, "...diverting research time, money, and other resources from more fruitful lines of investigation in order to pursue a theory that has no basis in biology."<sup id="cite_ref-Novella2010_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novella2010-65">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gorski2010_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorski2010-66">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another critic has argued that academic proponents of <a href="/wiki/Integrative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrative medicine">integrative medicine</a> sometimes recommend misleading patients by using known placebo treatments to achieve a placebo effect.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;n 15&#93;</a></sup> However, a 2010 survey of family physicians found that 56% of respondents said they had used a placebo in clinical practice as well. Eighty-five percent of respondents believed placebos can have both psychological and physical benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-Kerman2010_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kerman2010-176">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Integrative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrative medicine">Integrative medicine</a> has been criticized in that its practitioners, trained in science-based medicine, deliberately mislead patients by pretending <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebos</a> are not. "<a href="/wiki/Quackery#Criticism_of_quackery_in_academia" title="Quackery">quackademic medicine</a>" is a pejorative term used for <i><a href="/wiki/Integrative_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrative medicine">integrative medicine</a></i>, which medical professionals consider an infiltration of quackery into academic <a href="/wiki/Science-based_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Science-based medicine">science-based medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gorski2010_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorski2010-66">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An analysis of trends in the criticism of complementary and alternative medicine (CAM) in five prestigious American medical journals during the period of reorganization within medicine (1965\xe2\x80\x931999) was reported as showing that the medical profession had responded to the growth of CAM in three phases, and that in each phase, changes in the medical marketplace had influenced the type of response in the journals.<sup id="cite_ref-Winnick2005_2009_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Winnick2005_2009-177">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Changes included relaxed medical licensing, the development of managed care, rising consumerism, and the establishment of the USA Office of Alternative Medicine (later <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine">National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Integrative_Health" title="National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health">National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;n 16&#93;</a></sup> In the "condemnation" phase, from the late 1960s to the early 1970s, authors had ridiculed, exaggerated the risks, and petitioned the state to contain CAM; in the "reassessment" phase (mid-1970s through early 1990s), when increased consumer utilization of CAM was prompting concern, authors had pondered whether patient dissatisfaction and shortcomings in conventional care contributed to the trend; in the "integration" phase of the 1990s physicians began learning to work around or administer CAM, and the subjugation of CAM to scientific scrutiny had become the primary means of control.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_and_regulation">Use and regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Use and regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appeal">Appeal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Appeal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Miracle_Cure!%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_(8528312890).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg/220px-%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg/330px-%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg/440px-%22Miracle_Cure%21%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_%288528312890%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2550" data-file-height="3300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Miracle_Cure!%22_Health_Fraud_Scams_(8528312890).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alternative therapies often make bombastic claims, and frequently include anecdotes from healthy-looking individuals claiming successful treatment.</div></div></div>\n<p>Practitioners of complementary medicine usually discuss and advise patients as to available alternative therapies. Patients often express interest in mind-body complementary therapies because they offer a non-drug approach to treating some health conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Sobel2000_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sobel2000-180">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the social-cultural underpinnings of the popularity of alternative medicine, there are several psychological issues that are critical to its growth. One of the most critical is the placebo effect&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 a well-established observation in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-van_Deventer2008_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-van_Deventer2008-181">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>  Related to it are similar psychological effects, such as the will to believe,<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-182">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_bias" title="Cognitive bias">cognitive biases</a> that help maintain self-esteem and promote harmonious social functioning,<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-182">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Post_hoc,_ergo_propter_hoc" class="mw-redirect" title="Post hoc, ergo propter hoc">post hoc, ergo propter hoc</a></i> fallacy.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-182">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing">Marketing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Marketing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edzard_ernst.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Edzard_ernst.jpg/220px-Edzard_ernst.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Edzard_ernst.jpg/330px-Edzard_ernst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Edzard_ernst.jpg/440px-Edzard_ernst.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1972" data-file-height="1664" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edzard_ernst.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a>, a leading authority on scientific study of alternative treatments and diagnoses, and the first university professor of Complementary and Alternative Medicine. Here in 2012, promoting his book <i><a href="/wiki/Trick_or_Treatment" class="mw-redirect" title="Trick or Treatment">Trick or Treatment</a></i> co-written with <a href="/wiki/Simon_Singh" title="Simon Singh">Simon Singh</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The popularity of complementary &amp; alternative medicine (CAM) may be related to other factors that <a href="/wiki/Edzard_Ernst" title="Edzard Ernst">Edzard Ernst</a> mentioned in an interview in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i>:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Why is it so popular, then? Ernst blames the providers, customers and the doctors whose neglect, he says, has created the opening into which alternative therapists have stepped. "People are told lies. There are 40 million websites and 39.9 million tell lies, sometimes outrageous lies. They mislead cancer patients, who are encouraged not only to pay their last penny but to be treated with something that shortens their lives. "At the same time, people are gullible. It needs gullibility for the industry to succeed. It doesn\'t make me popular with the public, but it\'s the truth.<sup id="cite_ref-Con?_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Con?-183">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Offit" title="Paul Offit">Paul Offit</a> proposed that "alternative medicine becomes quackery" in four ways: by recommending against conventional therapies that are helpful, promoting potentially harmful therapies without adequate warning, draining patients\' bank accounts, or by promoting "magical thinking."<sup id="cite_ref-Offit2013_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offit2013-52">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lemon-906141.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lemon-906141.jpg/220px-Lemon-906141.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lemon-906141.jpg/330px-Lemon-906141.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lemon-906141.jpg/440px-Lemon-906141.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4842" data-file-height="3632" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lemon-906141.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Friendly and colorful images of herbal treatments may look less threatening or dangerous when compared to conventional medicine. This is an intentional marketing strategy.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_factors">Social factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Social factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Authors have speculated on the socio-cultural and psychological reasons for the appeal of alternative medicines among the minority using them <i>in lieu</i> of conventional medicine. There are several socio-cultural reasons for the interest in these treatments centered on the low level of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_literacy" title="Scientific literacy">scientific literacy</a> among the public at large and a concomitant increase in <a href="/wiki/Antiscience" title="Antiscience">antiscientific</a> attitudes and <a href="/wiki/New_age" class="mw-redirect" title="New age">new age</a> <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_182-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-182">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Related to this are vigorous <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketing</a><sup id="cite_ref-Weber_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weber-184">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> of extravagant claims by the alternative medical community combined with inadequate media scrutiny and attacks on critics.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_182-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-182">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein2001_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein2001-185">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is also an increase in <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theory" title="Conspiracy theory">conspiracy theories</a> toward conventional medicine and pharmaceutical companies, mistrust of traditional authority figures, such as the physician, and a dislike of the current delivery methods of scientific biomedicine, all of which have led patients to seek out alternative medicine to treat a variety of ailments.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein2001_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein2001-185">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Many patients lack access to contemporary medicine, due to a lack of private or public <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a>, which leads them to seek out lower-cost alternative medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-186">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Medical doctors are also aggressively marketing alternative medicine to profit from this market.<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weber-184">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Patients can be averse to the painful, unpleasant, and sometimes-dangerous <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect" title="Adverse effect">side effects</a> of biomedical treatments. Treatments for severe diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> infection have well-known, significant side-effects. Even low-risk medications such as <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a> can have potential to cause life-threatening <a href="/wiki/Anaphylactic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaphylactic">anaphylactic</a> reactions in a very few individuals. Many medications may cause minor but bothersome symptoms such as cough or upset stomach. In all of these cases, patients may be seeking out alternative treatments to avoid the adverse effects of conventional treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein_182-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein-182">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beyerstein2001_185-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beyerstein2001-185">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence_of_use">Prevalence of use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Prevalence of use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>Complementary and alternative medicine</b> (CAM) has been described as a broad domain of healing resources that encompasses all health systems, modalities, and practices and their accompanying theories and beliefs, other than those intrinsic to the politically dominant health system of a particular society or culture in a given historical period. CAM includes all such practices and ideas self-defined by their users as preventing or treating illness or promoting health and well-being. Boundaries within CAM and between the CAM domain and that of the dominant system are not always sharp or fixed.<sup id="cite_ref-Wieland2011_82-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieland2011-82">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (February 2013)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Alternative_medicine#Dubious" title="Talk:Alternative medicine">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>According to recent research, the increasing popularity of the CAM needs to be explained by moral convictions or lifestyle choices rather than by economic reasoning.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing nations</a>, access to essential medicines is severely restricted by lack of resources and <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>. <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">Traditional remedies</a>, often closely resembling or forming the basis for alternative remedies, may comprise primary healthcare or be integrated into the healthcare system. In Africa, traditional medicine is used for 80% of primary healthcare, and in developing nations as a whole over one-third of the population lack access to essential medicines.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_tradmed_fact_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_tradmed_fact-188">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some have proposed adopting a <a href="/wiki/Prize" title="Prize">prize</a> system to reward medical research.<sup id="cite_ref-Horrobin1986_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horrobin1986-189">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> However, public funding for research exists. Increasing the funding for research on alternative medicine techniques is the purpose of the <a href="/wiki/US_National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Alternative_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="US National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine">US National Center for Complementary and Alternative Medicine</a>. NCCIH and its predecessor, the Office of Alternative Medicine, have spent more than US$2.5 billion on such research since 1992; this research has largely not demonstrated the efficacy of alternative treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-$2.5_billion_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-$2.5_billion-172">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nccih_$_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nccih_$-190">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Atwood2003_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atwood2003-191">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>That alternative medicine has been on the rise "in countries where Western science and scientific method generally are accepted as the major foundations for healthcare, and \'evidence-based\' practice is the dominant paradigm" was described as an "enigma" in the Medical Journal of Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-Coulter2004_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coulter2004-193">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_US">In the US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: In the US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the United States, the 1974 <a href="/wiki/Child_Abuse_Prevention_and_Treatment_Act" title="Child Abuse Prevention and Treatment Act">Child Abuse Prevention and Treatment Act</a> (CAPTA) required that for states to receive federal money, they had to grant religious exemptions to <a href="/wiki/Child_neglect" title="Child neglect">child neglect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">abuse</a> laws regarding religion-based healing practices.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Thirty-one states have child-abuse religious exemptions.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The use of alternative medicine in the US has increased,<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1998_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1998-196">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> with a 50 percent increase in expenditures and a 25 percent increase in the use of alternative therapies between 1990 and 1997 in America.<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1998_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1998-196">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Americans spend many billions on the therapies annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Eisenberg1998_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1998-196">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Most Americans used CAM to treat and/or prevent musculoskeletal conditions or other conditions associated with chronic or recurring pain.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-186">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> In America, women were more likely than men to use CAM, with the biggest difference in use of <a href="/wiki/Terms_and_concepts_in_alternative_medicine#Mind-body_interventions" class="mw-redirect" title="Terms and concepts in alternative medicine">mind-body therapies</a> including prayer specifically for health reasons".<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-186">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, more than 37% of American hospitals offered alternative therapies, up from 27 percent in 2005, and 25% in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Warner2006_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2006-197">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AHA2008_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2008-198">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> More than 70% of the hospitals offering CAM were in urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-AHA2008_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2008-198">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A survey of Americans found that 88 percent thought that "there are some good ways of treating sickness that medical science does not recognize".<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Use of magnets was the most common tool in <a href="/wiki/Energy_medicine" title="Energy medicine">energy medicine</a> in America, and among users of it, 58 percent described it as at least "sort of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific</a>", when it is not at all scientific.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, at least 60 percent of US medical schools have at least some class time spent teaching alternative therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> "Therapeutic touch", was taught at more than 100 colleges and universities in 75 countries before the practice was debunked by a nine-year-old child for a school science project.<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_2002_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF_2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rosa_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosa-131">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence_of_use_of_specific_therapies">Prevalence of use of specific therapies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Prevalence of use of specific therapies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The most common CAM therapies used in the US in 2002 were prayer (45%), <a href="/wiki/Herbalism" title="Herbalism">herbalism</a> (19%), <a href="/wiki/Terms_and_concepts_in_alternative_medicine#Breathing_Meditation" class="mw-redirect" title="Terms and concepts in alternative medicine">breathing meditation</a> (12%), <a href="/wiki/Meditation_(alternative_medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Meditation (alternative medicine)">meditation</a> (8%), <a href="/wiki/Chiropractic_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiropractic medicine">chiropractic medicine</a> (8%), <a href="/wiki/Yoga_(alternative_medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yoga (alternative medicine)">yoga</a> (5\xe2\x80\x936%), <a href="/wiki/Body_work_(alternative_medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Body work (alternative medicine)">body work</a> (5%), <a href="/wiki/Terms_and_concepts_in_alternative_medicine#Diet-based_therapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Terms and concepts in alternative medicine">diet-based therapy</a> (4%), <a href="/wiki/Terms_and_concepts_in_alternative_medicine#Progressive_Relaxation" class="mw-redirect" title="Terms and concepts in alternative medicine">progressive relaxation</a> (3%), <a href="/wiki/Orthomolecular_medicine" title="Orthomolecular medicine">mega-vitamin therapy</a> (3%) and <a href="/wiki/Visualization_(cam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Visualization (cam)">Visualization</a> (2%)<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2004_186-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2004-186">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barnes2008_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2008-199">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Britain, the most often used alternative therapies were <a href="/wiki/Alexander_technique" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander technique">Alexander technique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aromatherapy" title="Aromatherapy">Aromatherapy</a>, Bach and other flower remedies, Body work therapies including massage, Counseling stress therapies, <a href="/wiki/Hypnotherapy" title="Hypnotherapy">hypnotherapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">Meditation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reflexology" title="Reflexology">Reflexology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shiatsu" title="Shiatsu">Shiatsu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayurvedic_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayurvedic medicine">Ayurvedic medicine</a>, Nutritional medicine, and <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESir_Walton:_Science_and_Technology_Committee2000_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESir_Walton:_Science_and_Technology_Committee2000-200">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Ayurvedic medicine remedies are mainly plant based with some use of animal materials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWujastyk2003_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWujastyk2003-201">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Safety concerns include the use of herbs containing toxic compounds and the lack of quality control in Ayurvedic facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Valiathan2006_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valiathan2006-125">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Saper2008_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saper2008-127">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service_(England)" title="National Health Service (England)">National Health Service (England)</a>, the most commonly used complementary and alternative medicines (CAM) supported by the NHS in the UK are: acupuncture, aromatherapy, chiropractic, homeopathy, massage, osteopathy and clinical hypnotherapy.<sup id="cite_ref-NHS_Careers_CAM_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Careers_CAM-202">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_palliative_care">In palliative care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: In palliative care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Complementary therapies are often used in <a href="/wiki/Palliative_care" title="Palliative care">palliative care</a> or by practitioners attempting to manage chronic pain in patients. Integrative medicine is considered more acceptable in the interdisciplinary approach used in palliative care than in other areas of medicine. "From its early experiences of care for the dying, palliative care took for granted the necessity of placing patient values and lifestyle habits at the core of any design and delivery of quality care at the end of life. If the patient desired complementary therapies, and as long as such treatments provided additional support and did not endanger the patient, they were considered acceptable."<sup id="cite_ref-Kellhear2003_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kellhear2003-203">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The non-pharmacologic interventions of complementary medicine can employ <a href="/wiki/Mind-body_intervention" class="mw-redirect" title="Mind-body intervention">mind-body</a> interventions designed to "reduce pain and concomitant mood disturbance and increase quality of life."<sup id="cite_ref-Menefee2005_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Menefee2005-204">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_alternative_medicine" title="Regulation of alternative medicine">Regulation of alternative medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regulation_and_prevalence_of_homeopathy" title="Regulation and prevalence of homeopathy">Regulation and prevalence of homeopathy</a></div><table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alternative_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small><i>(May 2018)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_(FDA_October_16,_2008).djvu" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_%28FDA_October_16%2C_2008%29.djvu/page1-220px-Use_Caution_With_Ayurvedic_Products_%28FDA_October_16%2C_2008%29.djvu.jpg"
